Jamison Brereton

61 (415)
Maruts
┼Ъy─Бv─Б┼Ыva ─Аtreya
19 verses: g─Бyatr─л, except anuс╣гс╣нubh 5 and satobr╠еhat─л 9

With its nineteen verses, this hymn is out of place by the normal rules of arrangement of the saс╣Гhit─Б. It also falls into several sections on thematic grounds (vss. 1тАУ4, 5тАУ10, 11тАУ16, 17тАУ19). However, at least the first half (1тАУ10) displays the cleverness and sly humor of ┼Ъy─Бv─Б┼Ыva, who names himself directly in verse 5 and punningly in verse 9, and perhaps represents a jeu dтАЩesprit of the poet, appended to the more solemn Marut hymns that precede it. Note that ┼Ъy─Бv─Б┼Ыva names himself only in the first of those hymns (V.52.1) and in this last one.

The Maruts are the clear subject of verses 1тАУ4 and 11тАУ16, and each of these sec tions is followed by a d─Бnastuti, or verses culminating in a d─Бnastuti: 10 and 17тАУ19. We therefore suggest that we are dealing here with two originally separate hymns, 1тАУ10 and 11тАУ19, and that the first at least bears clear marks of ┼Ъy─Бv─Б┼ЫvaтАЩs authorship.

Since the second of these is less interesting than the first, we will discuss it first here. The six verses (11тАУ16) treating the Maruts are fairly conventional. Verse 17 serves as a transition to the d─Бnastuti, addressed, oddly, to the goddess Night, who is urged to carry the poetтАЩs hymns to his patron, called D─Бrbhya here, and Rathav─лti in the following two verses. Both parts of the latter name are punned upon: ratha тАЬchariotтАЭ in verse 17 (тАЬlike a charioteerтАЭ), -v─лti тАЬpursuitтАЭ in verse 18 in the verb form (├бpa) veti тАЬgo off track,тАЭ and he is praised for his generosity in general terms (19). The first hymn is a much less staid affair. The four opening verses to the Maruts begin with questions about the Maruts and then in increasing detail about their horses and horse-tackle. And in verses 2c and 3b the Maruts are depicted as riding horseback (one of the very few pieces of evidence for this activity in the R╠еgveda); in verse 2 the reference is unremarkable, but in verse 3 the menтАЩs posture on the horse reminds the poet of women spreading their thighs in sex (or possibly childbirth, though this seems less likely). From this point on the hymn takes a sexually suggestive turn. In verse 4 the Maruts are sent off with тАЬlucky women,тАЭ and we move abruptly into the second section of the hymn.

That section ends with a real d─Бnastuti verse (10) in praise of a patron named Vaidada┼Ыvi. We might expect the four preceding verses (5тАУ9) to form part of the d─Бnastuti as well, and there are prominent d─Бnastuti-like elements in them, but they also subvert the d─Бnastuti model or provide a counter modelтАФnot least because they concern a woman and profess the unorthodox opinion that women can be superior to men. Verse 5 introduces an unidentified woman who тАЬgainsтАЭ the sorts of things poets usually receive in d─Бnastutis, using the root for тАЬgainтАЭ (san) that is standard in such contexts. (The notion that a woman would be in such a position is so unusual that most translators simply translate the word as if it means тАЬgiveтАЭтАФthough a woman who gives livestock is no more standard a figure than one who gains it.) From the second half of the verse it is clear that she must be the favored consort (perhaps even the тАЬFavorite WifeтАЭ of later Vedic ritual) of ┼Ъy─Бv─Б┼ЫvaтАЩs patron. The poet then seems to reflect (vss. 6тАУ8) on the qualities of women in general, in contrast to men, allowing that some women may actually be better than men, especially men of bad characterтАФand among those especially a stingy man (vss. 6, 8), who doesnтАЩt deserve to be called a man except in the technical matter of blood money (vs. 8). The tone in these verses is colloquial and conversational. After this seeming digression, with the subtle hint to his patron about the perils of stinginess, we return in verse 9 to a flesh-and-blood woman, who is working her wiles on the poet, who calls himself тАЬDuskyтАЭ (┼Ыy─Бva). The verse is an elaborate pun: on the one hand she is narrating the course of a race, and the тАЬtwo chestnutsтАЭ in the second half of the verse can be two horses given to the poet, тАЬstraining forwardтАЭ to win. But the tone is very intimate; the тАЬcourseтАЭ may be the progress of sexual intercourse between the girl and the poet, and the second half of the verse, using the same verb (v├н тИЪyam тАЬspread apartтАЭ) as in the first sexually suggestive passage in verse 4, can describe the тАЬspreading apartтАЭ of two female body parts. The girl in verse 9 thus appears to be part of the тАЬgiftтАЭ that ┼Ъy─Бv─Б┼Ыva received for his poem, and the complex set of double meanings in this verse are certainly a worthy тАЬpraise of this gift.тАЭ Is the girl in this verse the same as the one in verse 5? ┼Ъy─Бv─Б┼Ыva seems to imply this, but we will never know for sure.

Jamison Brereton Notes

Maruts For the structure of this hymn and its place in the series, see published introduction.

Although there is an itih─Бsa in the Bс╣Ыhaddevat─Б (V.50) that supposedly recounts the circumstances of ┼Ъy─Бv─Б┼ЫvaтАЩs composition, it was clearly constructed secondarily and fancifully on the basis of the Rigvedic text (see both Oldenberg and Geldner).

English translation:

тАЬWho are you, most excellent leadeers (of rites), who come one by one from a region exceedingly remote?тАЭ

Commentary by S─Бyaс╣Зa: с╣Ъgveda-bh─Бс╣гya

A wonderful old story: ─Б┼Ыcaryam pur─Бvс╣Ыttam ─Бhur─Бgamap─Бragaс╕е, those who have gone through the ─Бgamas have related a wonderful occurrence

Jamison Brereton

Who are you, men, the fairest ones, who one by one have driven here from the farthest distance?

Jamison Brereton Notes

Note the doubly marked superlative ┼Ыr├йс╣гс╣нhatama- (also I.113.12), which therefore ought to be rendered as тАЬthe most fairest.тАЭ It must be nom., not voc., because of its accent, since in a standard voc. phrase it would be unaccented flg.

unaccented naraс╕е.

It is somewhat notable that the Maruts are referred to ├йka-ekaс╕е тАЬone by one,тАЭ since they are usually not differentiated at all. _________ 01-02 These two vss. contain a fairly high percentage of the total number of RVic 2nd pl. active pfs., with the poorly characterized ending -a on the weak stem: ─Бyay├б (1b), ┼Ыeka, yaya (2b). Macdonell (VG p. 358) lists only twelve forms in all of Vedic (some of which have more than one token) of the 2nd pl. act. pf., and one of these (an─Бha) is better interpr. otherwise (see comm. ad VIII.48.5).

Griffith

Forth from a region most remote?

English translation:

тАЬWhere are your horses? where your reins? what is your capability? where are you going? The saddle is on the back (of the steeds), the bridle in their nostrils.тАЭ

Jamison Brereton

Where are your horses, where your reins? How have you been able? How have you driven here?
(Where is) the seat on their back, the bridle at their nostrils?

Jamison Brereton Notes

kath─Б╠Бcan be read thus or as kath─Б╠Б─Б╠Б, with the same preverb as in the univerbated ─Бyay├б in the rel. cl. of 1b. The Pp. does not read the preverb. It is possible that the variation between kath├бm in the first question of the p─Бda and kath─Б╠Б is meant to enable the preverb reading, and the published translation reflects that.

With Geldner and Renou I assume a gapped kv├а in c and in 3a. Witzel Got┼Н simply take them as questions without an explicit interrogative (тАЬIst ein Sattel auf einem R├╝cken тАж?тАЭ etc.). The difference is unimportant rhetorically.

Griffith

Where are your horses, where the reins? How came ye? how had ye the power?
Rein was on nose and seat on back.

Geldner

Wo sind eure Rosse, wo die Z├╝gel? Wie habt ihr das vermocht, wie seid ihr gekommen? Wo ist der Sattel auf dem R├╝cken, der Zaum in den N├╝stern der Rosse?

Grassmann

Wo sind die Ross’ und Z├╝gel euch? wie machtet ihr’s? wie kamt ihr her? auf R├╝cken [der Rosse] Sitz, in Nasen Zaum.

Elizarenkova

English translation:

тАЬThe goad is (applied) to their flanks; the drivers force them to spread their thighs apart, like women in bringing forth children.тАЭ

Jamison Brereton

(Where) the whip on their haunch? The men have spread apart their thighs (on horseback),
like wives at the making of sons.

Jamison Brereton Notes

For this vs. see published introduction.; as pointed out there, the simile in c can either refer to sex or to childbirth.

It is interesting that sakth─Б╠Бni is plural. Although there are plural тАШmenтАЩ (n├бraс╕е) and therefore twice as many thighs as men, in Classical Sanskrit it is my impression that the dual would ordinarily be used in such a situation, where a number of men each spread their two thighs. RV seems more flexible.

Jamison Brereton Notes

The final word of vs. 3 is j├бnayaс╕е, while the final word of the 1st hemistich of 4 is (bhadra-)j─Бnayaс╕е, showing the standard distribution of the тАШwifeтАЩ word, with simplex j├бni- and j─Бni- as 2nd cmpd. member. The distrib. here is complicated by the fact that 3c тАж n├б j├бnayaс╕е produces an unusual cadence of four shorts, which

*j─Б╠Бnayaс╕е would easily repair. But since the long-vowel variant never shows up as a simplex, we should presumably resist the temptation to emend.

Jamison Brereton

Go forth, heroes, you young bloods with lucky women,
so that you will be hot as fire.

Jamison Brereton Notes

The 2nd pl. impv. etana shows an unexpected full-grade root syllable in the Saс╣Гhit─Б text, but is read with metrically problematic but morphologically тАЬcorrectтАЭ zero-grade in the Pp (itana). Of course, such full-grade 2nd pl. impvs. are found elsewhere, but in this case I wonder if itтАЩs a secondary extraction from forms cmpded with the preverb ─Б╠Б, as in V.87.8, also a Marut hymn, with p─Бda-final ├йtana. See also disc. ad VI.42.2.

Jamison Brereton Notes

The final word of vs. 3 is j├бnayaс╕е, while the final word of the 1st hemistich of 4 is (bhadra-)j─Бnayaс╕е, showing the standard distribution of the тАШwifeтАЩ word, with simplex j├бni- and j─Бni- as 2nd cmpd. member. The distrib. here is complicated by the fact that 3c тАж n├б j├бnayaс╕е produces an unusual cadence of four shorts, which

*j─Б╠Бnayaс╕е would easily repair. But since the long-vowel variant never shows up as a simplex, we should presumably resist the temptation to emend.

рд╕рд╛ рддрд░рдиреНрддрдорд╣рд┐рд╖реА рдорд╣реНрдпрдВ рд╕рдирддреН рдкреНрд░рд╛рджрд╛рддреН ред рдХрд┐рдореНред рдЕрд╢реНрд╡реНрдпрдореН рдЕрд╢реНрд╡рд╛рддреНрдордХрдВ рддрддреНрд╕рдореВрд╣рд╛рддреНрдордХрдВ рд╡рд╛ рдкрд╢реБрдореН ред рдЬрд╛рддрд╛рд╡реЗрдХрд╡рдЪрдирдореН ред рдЙрдд рдЕрдкрд┐ рдЪ рдЧрд╡реНрдпрдВ рдЧреЛрд╕рдореВрд╣рд╛рддреНрдордХрдВ рдкрд╢реБрдВ рд╢рддрд╛рд╡рдпрдореН рдЕрдиреЗрдХреИрд░рд╡рд┐рднрд┐рд░реНрдпреБрдХреНрддрдВ рд╕рдирджрд┐рддрд┐ ред рд╕реЗрддреНрдпреБрдХреНрддрдВ рдХреЗрддреНрдпрд╛рд╣ ред рдпрд╛ рд╢реНрдпрд╛рд╡рд╛рд╢реНрд╡рд╕реНрддреБрддрд╛рдп рд╢реНрдпрд╛рд╡рд╛рд╢реНрд╡реЗрди рдордпрд╛ рд╕реНрддреБрддрд╛рдп рд╡реАрд░рд╛рдп рддрд░рдиреНрддрд╛рдп рджреЛрдГ рд╕реНрд╡рдХреАрдпрдВ рднреБрдЬрдореН рдЙрдкрдмрд░реНрдмреГрд╣рддреН рдЙрдкреЛрджрдпрдЪреНрдЫрджрд╛рд▓рд┐рдЩреНрдЧрдирд╛рдп рд╕рд╛ рд╕рдирджрд┐рддрд┐ ред рдпрддрдГ рднрд░реНрддреГрдкреНрд░реЗрдорд╛рд╕реНрдкрджрд╛ рддрд╕реНрдорд╛рджреЗрд╡ рдмрд╣реБ рдзрдирдВ рджрд╛рддреЗ рд╢рдХреНрддреЗрддреНрдпрд░реНрдердГ рее рее реирем рее

Wilson

English translation:

тАЬShe, (┼Ъa┼Ыiyas─л), who has thrown her arms round the hero Taranta, who was eulogized by ┼Ъy─Бv─Б┼Ыva, has given me cattle comprising horses, and cows, and hundreds of sheep.тАЭ

Jamison Brereton

She gains livestock in horses and cows and (livestock) consisting of a hundred sheepтАФ
the one who keeps plumping up her arm as a pillow for the hero praised by ┼Ъy─Бv─Б┼Ыva.

Jamison Brereton Notes

As noted in the published introduction, the standard tr. (now incl. Witzel Got┼Н) take s├бnat as meaning тАШgaveтАЩ (Geldner schenkte, Renou a donn├й, Witzel Got┼Н geschenkt hat), not тАШgainedтАЩ; Indeed Renou claims that the stem s├бna- means тАШgiveтАЩ in d─Бnastutis, but provides no parallels. I have found none in any of the occurrences of this stem, but by contrast quite a number of exx. of s├бna- in d─Бnastutis with the expected meaning тАШgainтАЩ (I.126.3, V.30.14; VIII.25.22, 24; 46.22, 29; 68.17; X.62.11). Note, however, the honorable exception of Klein (DGRV I.431) тАЬThat one has obtained [my italics] a herd consisting of horses тАжтАЭ; Grassmann likewise gives the verb in this passage its standard meaning тАШerlangenтАЩ. The only possible reason I can imagine that this array of skilled philologists resolutely turned their backs on the very clear evidence of the semantics of this root is that they couldnтАЩt imagine that a female could have won or gained these prizes – though, as noted in the published introduction, it would be just as anomalous for a female to give them. If I am right that the woman in question is a favored concubine, or even the Favorite Wife (V─Бv─Бt─Б) of later Vedic ritual, of ┼Ъy─Бv─Б┼ЫvaтАЩs royal patron, then he may have indulged her with a little gift at the time of the general distribution of bounty. She does, after all, perform services for him, as p─Бda d shows.

┼Ыat─Б╠Бvayam is almost universally interpr. as тАШconsisting of a hundred (/hundreds of) sheepтАЩ, with the 2nd member derived from ├бvi- (so Grassmann, Geldner, Renou, Klein; also AiG II.2.140 and II.1 Nachtr. 34), and certainly sheep fit nicely into a sequence with horses and cows. Witzel Got┼Н demur, suggesting that such a cmpd should rather be

*┼Ыat─Бvya- and proposing an alternate analysis on the model of c├бtur-vaya- тАШfourfoldтАЩ (2x) – hence тАЬlivestock in horses and cows a hundred fold.тАЭ However, the -vaya- of c├бtur-vaya- is not otherwise found in such cmpds and has no obvious source (see AiG II.2.906), and the long final vowel of the first member ┼Ыat─Б╠Б- would not be quite as easily explained (though stems like ┼Ыat─Б╠Бmagha- and ┼Ыat─Бvant- also show unetymological long vowels). I do think Witzel Got┼НтАЩs point is well-taken that *avaya- is an unlikely deriv. to ├бvi-, esp. since that stem shows тАЬclosedтАЭ inflection, but -vaya- тАШ- foldтАЩ has its own problems. I suggest rather that the cmpd orig. contained the wellattested deriv. avy├бya- тАШsheep-y, ovineтАЩ (hence *┼Ыat─Бvyaya-), which has undergone haplology of the first y. Note that there’s a lot of phononological play in these vss.

(sibilants and v’s, with a’s), inspired by the poet’s name ┼Ъy─Бva-a┼Ыva: 5a s─Б╠Б├б┼Ыvi yam …

b ┼Ыat─Б╠Бvayam … c ┼Ыy─Бv├б-a┼Ыva [or Holland & van Nooten Rig Veda editionтАЩs ┼Ыy─Бv─Б┼Ыu va] … 6a ┼Ы├б┼Ы─л╠Б yas─л … b тАж v├бsyas─л, which could have contributed to the deformation of the underlying cmpd. I would therefore keep the older meaning, but with a different analysis of the 2nd member.

Note also that the end of c, (-stut)─Бya y─Б╠Б, is nearly identical to the ends of 1b ─Бyay├б and 2b (kath)─Б╠Бyaya.

Griffith

May she gain cattle for her meed, hundreds of sheep and steeds and kine,
Who threw embracing arms around the hero whom gyavaiva praised.

Geldner

Sie schenkte Ro├Я- und Rinderherde nebst hundert Schafen, die dem von Syavasva gepriesenen Manne den Arm als Kissen unterlegt.

Grassmann

Es empfange einen Viehstand, reich an Rossen und Rindern, hundert Schafe z├дhlend, die, welche ihren Arm dem von ^javaa^va gepriesenen Helden unterbreitet.

Elizarenkova

English translation:

тАЬ┼Ъa┼Ы─лyasi, though a feminine le, is more excellent than a man who reverences not the gods, nor bestows wealth.тАЭ

Commentary by S─Бyaс╣Зa: с╣Ъgveda-bh─Бс╣гya

Though a feminine le: uta tv─Б str─л ┼Ыa┼Ы─лyas─л: tv─Б = eka, one

Jamison Brereton

Now a woman can be more reliable, better than a man
if heтАЩs estranged from the gods, ungenerousтАФ

Jamison Brereton Notes

The comparative ┼Ы├б┼Ы─лyas- is attested only twice in the RV, here and in IV.32.3. Though they have different senses in the two passages (тАШmore reliableтАЩ here, тАШmore numerousтАЩ there, at least by my interpr.), the semantic dev. from the positive ┼Ы├б┼Ыvant- тАШregularly recurring, in unbroken sequence, each and everyтАЩ to the two senses of the comp. is fairly straightforward. Here, regularly recurring > constant > reliable. In VIII.1.34 this development takes a sexual turn, with a ┼Ы├б┼Ыvat─л n─Б╠Бr─л being one who is тАЬever readyтАЭ for sex (in my interpr.), and a sexual nuance is not ruled out in our passage either – though here the emphasis is on her dependable qualities compared to the unsatisfactory man.

Wilson

English translation:

тАЬFor she discerns one who suffers pain, one who is in want, or one desirous (of any thing), and directs her mind towards the gods.тАЭ

Jamison Brereton

While she pays attention to a famished man, or to a thirsting one, or to one who has desires [/lust],
and sets her own mind upon the gods.

Jamison Brereton Notes

The relativization is loose, at least in the Eng. rendering. Literally it should be тАЬMore reliable than a man is a woman who discerns the thirsting one тАж,тАЭ etc.

The lexeme v├н тИЪj├▒─Б means тАШdiscern, recognizeтАЩ, and a full rendering of the implication of these VPS would be something like тАЬwho recognizes (that a particular man) is famished тАжтАЭ – the idea presumably being that, having recognized their plights, she then sees to them, each in his own need. I have folded this two-step process into тАШpay attention toтАЩ. If she does see to the needs of each, the third in the series k─Бm├нnam тАШthe desirous / lusting oneтАЩ again introduces the sexual: she feeds, clothes, and has sex with the three in turn, at least by my interpr. The standard tr.

have a tendency to attenuate the third term – e.g., Klein (DGRV I.432) тАЬthe one desiring (aid)тАЭ – but in this saucy hymn I think sex is never far from the surface. I do have to admit, however, that k─Бm├нn- is not otherwise used of sexual desire, but has a more general application, incl. to the Maruts in this same cycle (V.53.16).

Jamison Brereton Notes

As indicated in the published translation, I consider these vss. a digression reflecting on the character of women in general, motivated by the introduction of the winning woman in vs. 5.

She who discerns the weak and worn, the man who thirsts and is in want
She sets her mind upon the Gods.

Wilson

English translation:

тАЬAnd, eulogizing (him), I proclaim that the man, her (other) half is, (as it were), uncommended, for he is ever the same in munificent donations.тАЭ

Commentary by S─Бyaс╣Зa: с╣Ъgveda-bh─Бс╣гya

Her other half: nemaс╕е is the term of the text; liс╣н, half as it is said in the Sс╣Гс╣Ыti, ardham ┼Ыar─лrasya bh─Бry─Б, wife is the half of the body (of the husband)

Jamison Brereton

And some other guy, a niggard not deserving praise, (may be) called a тАЬman,тАЭ
but he is only equivalent (to a man) in the matter of wergeld.

Jamison Brereton Notes

On va├нradeya-, lit. тАШwhat is to be given for a man/heroтАЩ, as Wergeld or blood money, see Roth (ZDMG 41: 672-76) and Macdonell-Keith Vedic Index s.v. vaira.

Another indirect ref. to the same phenomenon is found in II.32.4 v─лr├бс╣Г ┼Ыat├бd─Бyam тАЬa hero (for whom) a hundred (cows) are to be given.тАЭ In our case the person in question is hardly worth the name тАШmanтАЩ and would be better off dead than alive, since his relatives would still receive the standard recompense for a v─лr├б, whatever his personal failings had been. It is telling that he is called a paс╣З├н- тАШniggardтАЩ, a reminder to ┼Ъy─Бv─Б┼ЫvaтАЩs patron that stinginess is a grave flaw.

рдЙрдд рдЕрдкрд┐ рдЪ рд╢реНрдпрд╛рд╡рд╛рдп рд╢реНрдпрд╛рд╡рд╛рд╢реНрд╡рд╛рдп рдореЗ рдорд╣реНрдпрдореН рдЕрд░рдкрддреН рдЕрд▓рдкрддреН ред рд╕реНрдкрд╖реНрдЯрдорд╛рдЪрд╖реНрдЯ ред рдХрд┐рдореН ред рд╡рд░реНрддрдирд┐рдВ рдорд╛рд░реНрдЧрдореН ред рдХреАрджреГрд╢реА рд╕рд╛ ред рдпреБрд╡рддрд┐рдГ рдкреНрд░рд╡реГрддреНрддрдпреМрд╡рдирд╛ рдкреНрд░рддрд┐ рдордордиреНрджреБрд╖реА рдкреНрд░рддрд┐рдореЛрджрдорд╛рдирд╛ ред рдХрд╕реНрдореИ ред рджреАрд░реНрдШрдпрд╢рд╕реЗ рдкреНрд░рднреВрддрд╛рдиреНрдирд╛рдп рдкреНрд░рднреВрддрдХреАрд░реНрддрдпреЗ рд╡рд╛ рдкреБрд░реБрдореАрд│реНрд╣рд╛рдп рдПрддрдиреНрдирд╛рдордХрд╛рдп рдкреНрд░рднреВрддрдЧреГрд╣рд╛рдп рд╡рд┐рдкреНрд░рд╛рдп рее тАШ рдХреНрд░рд┐рдпрд╛рд░реНрдереЛрдкрдкрджрд╕реНрдп рдЪ рдХрд░реНрдордгрд┐ рд╕реНрдерд╛рдирд┐рдирдГ ’ ( рдкрд╛. рд╕реВ. реи. рей. резрек ) рдЗрддрд┐ рдЪрддреБрд░реНрдереА рее рдкреБрд░реБрдореАрд│реНрд╣рдВ рдкреНрд░рд╛рдкреНрддреБрдорд┐рддреНрдпрд░реНрдердГ ред рддрджрд░реНрдердВ рд░реЛрд╣рд┐рддрд╛ рд░реЛрд╣рд┐рддрд╡рд░реНрдгрд╛рд╡рд╢реНрд╡реМ рд╢рд╢реАрдпрд╕реНрдпрд╛ рджрддреНрддреМ рд╡рд┐ рдпреЗрдорддреБрдГ ред рдзреГрддрд╡рдиреНрддрд╛рд╡рд┐рддреНрдпрд░реНрдердГ ред рдпрд╛ рдЕрд╢реНрд╡рд╛рднреНрдпрд╛рдВ рдпреБрдХреНрддреЗ рд░рдереЗ рд╕реНрдерд╛рдкрдпрд┐рддреНрд╡рд╛ рдкреБрд░реБрдореАрд│реНрд╣рдЧреГрд╣рд╕реНрдп рдорд╛рд░реНрдЧрдВ рдкреНрд░рджрд░реНрд╢рдпрдиреНрддреА рдкреБрд░рддреЛ рдЧрддрд╛ рд╕реИрд╡ рдкреБрдВрд╕реЛ рднрд╡рддрд┐ рд╡рд╕реНрдпрд╕реАрддрд┐ рдкреВрд░реНрд╡рддреНрд░ рд╕рдВрдмрдиреНрдзрдГ рее

Wilson

English translation:

тАЬYoung and affable, she has explained to me, ┼Ъy─Бv─Б┼Ыva, the road, and two ruddy horses have borne me to the valiant and renowned Purum─лс╕╖ha.тАЭ

Jamison Brereton

But a young woman, having beguiled (me), whispered the course to me, тАЬDuskyтАЭ [=┼Ъy─Бv─Б┼Ыva], face to face.
The two chestnuts spread apart [/strained forward] for the
much-rewarded one, for the inspired poet of lasting gloryтАФ

Jamison Brereton Notes

On this vs. see published introduction. As detailed there, I take the vs. as an elaborate pun. On the one hand a young woman (yuvat├нс╕е) is narrating the course of a race (or perhaps the progress of the horses that are to be given to the poet as his prize) to the poet – thus the word vartan├н- тАШcourse, trackтАЩ in b and the two chestnut (horses) (r├│hit─Б) in c. (Note also that ┼Ыy─Бv├б- is not only the short form of the poetтАЩs name but also, more usually, a horse color term.) But a more intimate scene is signalled by her low voice (тИЪrap is ordinarily used of murmuring or muttering; see the esp. telling passage in the Yama/Yam─л hymn X.10.4, where it is contrasted with тИЪvad), her use of a nickname тАЬDuskyтАЭ (┼Ыy─Бv├б-), and esp. the pf. part. mamand├║с╣г─л тАШhaving intoxicated, beguiled, seducedтАЩ (I follow K├╝mmel [366-67] in taking the pf. as transitive and supplying тАШmeтАЩ as its obj.). And the whole vs. can be read as a description of sexual intercourse. The тАШcourseтАЩ would be the course of the sex act, and the two ruddy ones in c can be any paired female body parts; given the use of the same verb v├н тИЪyam here as in 3b, where men spread their thighs, they may be thighs, but the labia and breasts are also possible. Both Geldner and Renou take the dat. purum─лс╕╖h─Б╠Бya in c as a PN and therefore not coreferential with ┼Ыy─Бv─Б╠Бya in b. But it makes more sense to interpr. purum─лс╕╖h├б- in its full lexical sense тАШhaving many rewardsтАЩ (so also Witzel Got┼Н), describing the poet (v├нpr─Бya in d) who has received his dakс╣гiс╣З─Б from his patron and who is in fact ┼Ъy─Бv─Б┼Ыva – and implying that part of this dakс╣гiс╣З─Б is the seductive woman in this very vs. On Purum─лс╕Нha in later Vedic see comm. on the next vs. As a number of others have noted (e.g., Hoffmann, Injunk. 150), though the Pp. reads arapat, the actual form may be the injunctive rapat; the PpтАЩs augment is not realized metrically or in the transmitted text. This doesnтАЩt affect the interpr. one way or the other. Though Grassmann lists pr├бti as a preverb with rapat, this lexeme is not otherwise found (in all of Skt., if Mon-Wms is to be believed), and given the distance between the two words and what would be an unusual position for a preverb in tmesis (p─Бda-initial but in the p─Бda after the verb), I think it unlikely that they are to be construed together. I take pr├бti as adverbial, reinforcing the intimacy of the scene, hence my тАШface to faceтАЩ.

English translation:

тАЬWho, the son of Vidada┼Ыva, has given me a hundred (head) of cattle, and, like Taranta, many precious gifts.тАЭ

Jamison Brereton

Whoever will give me a hundred milk-cows as Vaidada┼Ыvi (did), (he is) like a victor in his munificence.

Jamison Brereton Notes

My interpr. of the d─Бnastuti vs. proper differs from the standard, which has in my opinion been unduly affected by the later Vedic rationalization of the passage.

Both JB (I.151) and PB (XIII.7.12) briefly narrate a tale in which Purum─лс╕Нha and Taranta figure as the two sons of Vaidada┼Ыvi, but as we just saw, purum─лс╕╖h├б- is better taken in its literal sense. I also take tarant├б- not as a PN but in lexical value. In either case it is likely a thematization of the pres. participle t├бrant- (cf. AiG II.2.211), showing the same accent shift as AV j─лvant├б- from j─л╠Бvant-, and it should mean тАШovercoming, victoriousтАЩ ├а тАШvictorтАЩ. By contrast I do think va├нdada┼Ыvi- is a PN, but a speaking name, as the vс╣Ыddhi deriv. of *vid├бd-a┼Ыva- тАШfinding / acquiring horsesтАЩ (cf. vid├бd-vasu-), an appropriate name for a patron. It is worth noting that both names ┼Ъy─Бv─Б┼Ыva and Vaidada┼Ыvi have Iranian counterparts; see Mayrhofer Personennamen s.vv.

How to distribute y├бс╕е and y├бth─Б and whether to interpr. the latter as a subordinator or a simile marker are the syntactic problems in this vs., and diff. tr.

have diff. solutions. I take y├бс╕е as marking a generalizing rel. cl. (тАШwhoever тАжтАЩ) and y├бth─Б as a simile marker, with c a nominal cl. functioning as the main clause to ab.

The verb in b, d├бdat is a short-vowel subjunctive (тАЬwhoever will give тАжтАЭ) but can also be interpr. as a thematized injunc., which in turn can be read with Vai┼Ыvada┼Ыvi (тАЬas V. didтАЭ). If I had had the courage of my convictions, тАЬdidтАЭ would not be in parens. in the published translation

English translation:

тАЬThose (Maruts) who are brought hither by swift horses, drinking the inembriating juice, receive here gratification.тАЭ

Jamison Brereton

(The Maruts), who drive themselves with their swift horses, drinking the exhilarating honey,
here they have acquired their fame.

Jamison Brereton Notes

As indicated in the published introduction, this vs. marks the beginning of the second, more conventional hymn in this composite, at least in my analysis.

Technically speaking тАШhorsesтАЩ should be in parens.

It is not clear if ─лm has its usual acc. function: it could anticipate m├бdhu in b, though that seems somewhat distant and that participial phrase is otherwise fully contained in b; it could function as a reflexive marker (тАЬdrive themselvesтАЭ); or it may be one of the rare instances of functionless ─лm.

I have silently depluralized ┼Ыr├бv─Бс╣Гsi, since тАШfamesтАЩ is not English. Perhaps the point here is that each Marut has his own ┼Ыr├бvas, though, since they are seldom distinguished, this may be overthinking it.

Griffith

They who are borne by rapid steeds, drinking the meath that gives delight,
They have attained high glories here.

Geldner

Die mit schnellen Rossen fahren, den berauschenden Met trinken, die haben sich dabei Ruhm erworben.

Grassmann

Die fahren mit der Rosse Schar, den Meth einschl├╝rfend, der berauscht, ] empfingen hier viel herrliches.

Wilson

English translation:

тАЬThey by whose glory heaven and earth are surpassed; who shine splendid in their chariots like the radiant (sun) in the heaven above.тАЭ

Jamison Brereton

With whose splendor they flash through the two world-halves on their chariots, like the bright ornament in heaven above.

Jamison Brereton Notes

This vs. has produced more consternation than it deserves, because of the reluctance of many interpr. to allow y├йс╣г─Бm to be coreferential with the subj. of vibhr─Б╠Бjante. In these interpr., if the subj. of that verb is the Maruts, then another referent for y├йс╣г─Бm must be found; if y├йс╣г─Бm refers to the Maruts, then another subj. of the verb must be found. See OldenbergтАЩs lengthy disc., which includes the differing suggestions of numerous scholars (incl. also interpr. y├йс╣г─Бm as *y├й eс╣г─Бm) but doesnтАЩt reach firm conclusions. Of modern interpr., Renou takes y├йс╣г─Бm as the Maruts and makes r├│das─л the subj. of the verb: тАЬles Deux Mondes brillent au loinтАЭ; in a fractured sense this goes back to Max M├╝llerтАЩs tr. in SBE 32, but M├╝ller emended the verb to

*bhr─Б╠Бjate and took r├│das─л as sg., whereas Renou mentions no such emendation and seems to be taking r├│das─л as dual, which will certainly not work grammatically with a pl. verb. Witzel Got┼Н seem to have accepted the *y├й eс╣г─Бm interpr. Geldner, surprisingly, is more relaxed and allows coreference under the rubric of тАЬder reflexive Gebrauch des Relativs,тАЭ which seems perfectly plausible to me: they flash with their own splendour.

The тАЬbright ornamentтАЭ of c is the sun: rukm├б- is so used elsewhere in clearer context (VI.51.1, VII.63.4).

Griffith

They by whose splendour both the worlds are over-spread they shine on cars
As the gold gleams above in heaven.

Wilson

English translation:

тАЬThat company of Maruts, ever young, riding in bright chariots, irreproachable, auspicious, motive, unobstructed.тАЭ

Jamison Brereton

This youthful Marutian flock with its glittering chariot is not to be scorned;
charging to beauty it is unrepulsable.

Jamison Brereton Notes

I have taken the two negated adjectives at the end of b and c, ├бnedyaс╕е and ├бpratiс╣гkutaс╕е, as predicated, in contrast to all the standard tr. There is, of course, no way to tell.

Griffith

That Marut band is ever young, borne on bright cars, unblamable,
Moving to victory, checked by none.

Geldner

Das war die jugendliche Marutschar mit schreckhaftem Wagen, tadellos, auf der Prunkfahrt begriffen, unaufhaltsam.

Grassmann

Die jugendliche Marutschar, die wagenrasche, treffliche, zum Prunke eilend ungehemmt.

Elizarenkova

English translation:

тАЬDesirous of praise, you are the guides (to happiness) of the man who propitiates (you) by this pious rite; you are bearers of invocations to the sacrifice.тАЭ

Jamison Brereton

You, who seek admiration, are those who lead the mortal forth with an insight right to the point,
are those who listen to his invocations on your journey.

Jamison Brereton Notes

Whose dh─л╠Б- is it? Geldner, Renou, and Witzel Got┼Н all assume it is the mortalтАЩs (e.g., Geldner тАЬIhr тАж f├╝hret den Sterblichen recht nach seinem SinnтАЭ), but the placement of the phrase itth─Б╠Бdhiy─Б╠Бright after praс╣Зet─Б╠Бraс╕е and at some distance from m├бrtam at least weakly suggests that it is the MarutsтАЩs. Since, as I have had occasion to remark many times, the Maruts sometimes have the role of verbal praisers and sometimes are the recipients of the same, I think both readings are possible and the poet may have wanted it to remain ambiguous. TichyтАЩs tr. (-tar-stems, 300-301) seems to attribute the dh─л╠Б- to the Maruts or at least remains neutral: тАЬIhr bringt den Menschen vorw├дrts тАж recht mit Bedacht.тАЭ Note that in the cmpd y─Бma-h┼лti- it is the MarutsтАЩ journeys (y─Б╠Бma-, 1st member) but the mortalтАЩs invocations (-h┼лti-, 2nd member).

And hearers when he cries for help.

English translation:

тАЬBear to D─Бrbhya, oс╕е night, turning away (from me to him), this my eulogy (of the Maruts); convey my praises, goddess, as a charioteer (conveys the contents of his vehicle to their destination).тАЭ

Commentary by S─Бyaс╣Зa: с╣Ъgveda-bh─Бс╣гya

Oh night: upon concluding the praises of the Maruts and thus having attained the rank of a с╣Ыс╣гi, ┼Ъy─Бv─Б┼Ыva summons the night, here called, Urmya, to convey the intelligence to Rathav─лti, who gives him his daughter, with many valuable presents, but at the end of the ceremony the с╣Ыс╣гi departd for the woods to resume his austerities

Jamison Brereton

This praise of mine, o Night, carry off to D─Бrbhya,
(my) hymns (carry away) like a charioteer, o goddess.

Jamison Brereton Notes

I do not understand why the goddess Night acts as the intermediary between the poet and his patron; see GeldnerтАЩs n. 17-19, which doesnтАЩt seem to me entirely satisfactory.

Jamison Brereton Notes

As noted in the published introduction, the patronтАЩs name Rathav─лti (тАШpursuit of the chariotтАЩ?) in 18, 19 is punned upon. In 17 (before he is explicitly mentioned, though the patronymic D─Бrbhya would presumably evoke him) Night is asked to carry the hymns тАЬlike a charioteerтАЭ (rath─л╠Бr iva); in 18 the 2nd cmpd. member -v─лti- is derived from the same root тИЪv─л тАШpursueтАЩ that supplies the verb ├бpa veti тАШgoes off trackтАЩ.

English translation:

тАЬAnd say on my behalf to Rathav─лti, when the libation is poured out, my love (for your daughter) does not depart.тАЭ

Jamison Brereton

And then speak for me thus to Rathav─лti who has pressed the soma: тАЬMy desire does not go off track.тАЭ

Jamison Brereton Notes

The Rathav─лti phrase is in the loc.; the published translation renders it as if the addressee, which is technically incorrect. It is either a loc. absol. without participle or a тАЬchez/beiтАЭ expression: тАЬspeak for me thus when R. (is present) / at RтАЩs.тАЭ Since the net result is the same, I have kept the easier-to-parse addressee tr.

17-19 ...{Loading}...
Jamison Brereton Notes

As noted in the published introduction, the patronтАЩs name Rathav─лti (тАШpursuit of the chariotтАЩ?) in 18, 19 is punned upon. In 17 (before he is explicitly mentioned, though the patronymic D─Бrbhya would presumably evoke him) Night is asked to carry the hymns тАЬlike a charioteerтАЭ (rath─л╠Бr iva); in 18 the 2nd cmpd. member -v─лti- is derived from the same root тИЪv─л тАШpursueтАЩ that supplies the verb ├бpa veti тАШgoes off trackтАЩ.

English translation:

тАЬThis opulent Rathav─лti dwells upon the (banks of the) Gomat─л (river), and has his home on (the skirts of) the (Him─Бlaya) mountains.тАЭ

Commentary by S─Бyaс╣Зa: с╣Ъgveda-bh─Бс╣гya

Gomat─л river: Gomat─лс╕е = having water, rivers, being in the plural ral; it may be a river which rises in the first range of the Him─Бlayas, or, it may be the river rising in Kulu, a feeder of the Beyaс╕е or Vy─Бsa

Jamison Brereton

This Rathav─лti dwells in peace, a bounteous patron throughout the cow-rich (clans) [/along the Gomat─л River],
set back among the mountains.

Jamison Brereton Notes

Geldner and Renou take g├│mat─лс╕е as a ref. to the Gomat─л river; Witzel Got┼Н to тАЬcow-rich lands.тАЭ I think itтАЩs likely a pun, and I supply v├н┼Ыaс╕е тАШclansтАЩ for one half of the pun.

[V.62-78 JPB]
